8 The Arecibo Observatory Is More Than Just a Telescope | WIRED The Arecibo Observatory in Puerto Rico was a famous astronomical facility known for its massive 305-meter radio telescope, which was the world's largest for decades before its collapse in December 2020. Following the collapse, the observatory was permanently closed to the public, and the telescope will not be rebuilt. The site is now being converted into an educational center, the Arecibo Center for STEM Education and Research (ACSER), to continue its legacy of science, technology, engineering, and math (STEM) education.   Observatory history and significance Construction and operation: The observatory was built between 1960 and 1963 and was the site of many important astronomical discoveries, including finding exoplanets and studying gravitational waves. Cultural impact: It was featured in popular media, such as the films Contact and GoldenEye.  Collapse and aftermath Collapse: In late 2020, two separate cable failures led to the collapse of the observatory's 900-ton receiver platform onto the dish below on December 1, 2020.  Reasons for collapse: The collapse was caused by long-term cable failures, with a report citing the "long-term zinc creep-induced failure" as the root cause. The initial damage from Hurricane Maria exacerbated the issues with the support cables.  Future plans: In October 2022, the National Science Foundation (NSF) announced it would not rebuild the telescope due to the cost and safety concerns.  New educational facility: The site is being developed into an educational center called ACSER, which aims to continue the observatory's legacy of STEM education and workforce development.

I spoke with my professor who was working on a R1 academic team tasked to analyze the failures in the collapse of this telescope.  His team's leading theory is that the condensed radio waves of the telescope can cause a faint electrical current to be generated along the cables nearby. It isn't much, but it doesn't need to be. These electrical currents would have decades to work, slowly weakening connection points until the rated strength is a thing of the past.  10:17 I don't think many realize just how crazy the failure rate at 62% rated strength is. When stress testing materials you generally set the rated strength WELL BELOW the actual strength it can tolerate, sometimes being half of what it can handle. It is akin to a cable that normally breaks at 200lbs eventually breaking at just 60lbs. Without a very good explanation as to how it became so weak the difference is staggering.

Now I imagine that there might have been some financial aspect to it, but on racing yachts we replace our standing rigging (the rigging wires and turnbuckles and fittings etc) at least every year. Cruising yachts tend to leave it for 5 to 10 years before replacing their rigs. As far as I know, the rigging wire on a yacht has NEVER failed (at least I‚Äôve never heard of it) It is always, without fail, the fittings on either end that go first. My point being that regular replacement of the wires and their associated fittings  could have avoided this specific incident. That does mean, however, that something else would have given way. This would also incur a pretty big cost. The last time I had my rig redone it was in the vicinity of $35000, which is for a boat nowhere near even half as big as they get. I can‚Äôt imagine the cost to replace these wires every year or few years but it would have to be in the millions. Not sure how much the whole thing cost to build in the first place but there‚Äôs a chance that over its lifetime, more would have been spent on the wires than anything else.

I'm not an engineer just a backyard mechanic, the weakest point of anything is where it's terminus is, for example a vehicle exhibiting electrical issues will most likely have a bad power or ground cable failure, when you inspect where the ground connects to the body you'll find corrosion and the wires will be able to be pulled out with little effort, these massive cables are no different, people don't realize how destructive being near the can be on steel especially, many years of constant exposure to salt in the atmosphere will weaken and destroy metal. A vehicle out west where there is hardly any rain and no use of salt on roads will last indefinitely because they don't rust, contrast that with a vehicle in the Midwest or east where they salt the roads heavily in winter some vehicles that are only five years old are ready for the junk yard, some places with a lot of dirt roads salt them year round to keep the dust down, these cables were simply past their sell by date.

„Ç¥„Éº„É´„Éá„É≥„Ç¢„Ç§
The cables appeared dry, I couldn't see any rope oil, which normally appears as dry grease between the strands on the outside surface.  Spelter sockets are fitted with lubrication points, this is either carried out manually, or it may be supplied by a lube line, or an automatic lubricator, which is a small pot of oil with a battery driven pump that injects a small amount of oil periodically.   The cables nod at the sockets, if the strands within the cables aren't lubricated, stress corrosion cracking will occur, causing the failure of individual strands, that can reduce the cables service life by more than 50%.   Typically this type of cable is inspected by radiography, but it isn't always effective as it can't see the broken strands within the socket.  If the ends of the strands protrude from the zinc casting, and are ground flat, they can be inspected ultrasonically, this is the most effective method of monitoring this type of cable, which is called a bridge strand cable.

Cables are supposed to use a 5:1 safety factor. 2:1 is criminally negligent. As cables age they lose strength. That‚Äôs why 5:1 is the US national standard. It has been the standard for over a century. The only way it could be built like this is by the department of defense who have exempted themselves from all safety standards. I am a professional rigger with over forty years of experience, starting in my teens. I‚Äôm over sixty now. If anyone had told me that it had only a 2:1 safety margin when it was built, I would‚Äôve thought they were out of their minds. The fact that it stayed up as long as it did is a testament to how well it was built. And bridges are not built with a 3:1 safety factor, they‚Äôre built with a 5:1 safety factor and so are elevators. Hydraulic lifts don‚Äôt have a safety factor as such, they have a rated pressure, beyond which the pump simply shuts down. Check valves keep the cylinder from draining. They are rated at 5:1 but it doesn‚Äôt directly correspond.

Degradation fail cascade: mainly caused by maintenance issues, funding, and improper amendments to the existing structure. There is rust everywhere, as this is far from an arid environment. The entire dish could have been utilized with material with "smarter" properties (to borrow from modern buzz-words). They could have been made lighter, catch less air, suffer less thermal strain due to reflective properties, used an ultra-slick/hydrophobic coating for low maintenance/self-cleaning properties, been able to develop any level of solar properties (likely just PV panels as focused solar would likely be in direct contradiction to the intended purpose of the structure), or utilized any kind of more modern paint additives or surface treatment that simulate more natural weathering with little to no degradation to physical properties. The fact that the monoliths were never utilized for any form of solar or wind power is even more inexcusable. On top of that, you have a now sheltered understory for nursing or rearing high-production agriculture of any sort underneath. If you never supplement the income of useful structures like these, they will be forced to survive almost solely on subsidies and philanthropy, which are both scrutinized by those that don't understand or recognize the value of scientific data or discovery. Much smarter and more passive connection and bearing systems with more unique materials/ fab methods AND better parameter monitoring is available today. It would serve to have mariners/machinists staffed that are familiar with the maintenance of cable-stay systems for a tensegrity structure like this (as proven in some of the pioneer structures of its kind in the suspension bridges and Madison Square Garden of NYC). Rigging hasn't changed fundamentally nor physically, but the technologies we have that are able to have agency over these fundamentals truly have progressed considerably. The reverberations of an incalculable amount of small vibrations on such a structure are not to be taken lightly, as the peak resonance of such dynamic maximum forces can be orders of magnitude past initial calculations. Modern digital twin and AI-assisted structure generation (termite-like structures allowing the wind from any of the monoliths to act like the plenums in the cooling towers of Baghdad would allow for all the pneumatics of the structure to be powerless -- steam and further pressure could easily be obtained via ram pumps and trompes for virtually free as well) would be trivial to implement in even the simplest and seemingly least impactful areas of the structure/terrain for minimal cost. Lots of room to hybridize such structures in the future!

The likely cause is:  People underestimate how corrosive an environment the Tropics are.  The Zinc and Spelter socket fails spectacularly as steel rusts between the zinc and the wire.  You get a steel wire with a rust layer surrounded by zinc -- rust doesn't hold much load at all.  You have to slather the cable and spelter joint with water repellant oil yearly to keep water from sucking in just from thermal cycling.

I never finished my degree, so my input should probably be disregarded. However, I'll spew my nonsense anyways. When I design, build, modify, or work on something, I think about future serviceability. If the upper portion was supported by cable stayed towers in the same manner, but had winches on all three, would that not help prevent this kind of thing? Obviously a lot more cost added, but would allow for extensive maintenance in the future. With something of this significance, you would think the cost/benefit ratio would be skewed heavily. Even if it was a single winch mechanism per column vs one per cable, which would mean you have to lower the entire structure for service, would that not end up easing up complications of poor weather or major refits (like the gregorian dome addition)?

I can tell you what *REALLY* happened to Arecibo. In 50s US intelligence noticed that you can intercept radio signals bounced of various objects and it very good conditions it is possible to learn locations of various missile tracking radars in USSR. And then somebody had a bright idea that the Moon is one of those objects that overflies Russia once a month and can be used to intercept signals. So suddenly US military became super, super interested in radio astronomy. Mountains of money were poured into building as many and as powerful radio dishes as possible, including Arecibo. These were not designed to stand the test of time -- supposed to be erected very quickly and stand for a relatively short time at a very high upkeep cost per typical military strategy. Once US got enough satellites Arecibo suddenly was not so hot for military, there was no need to catch radio signals bounced off the Moon anymore, the military financing dried up and civilian administrators were left with a huge telescope and a very small budget that had absolutely no chance to cover for astronomical (sic!) requirements to keep it up in good shape. Now you see, there were only two possible ends. They would have to tear it down or it would collapse on its own. Nobody wanted to make the decision to tear it down so it collapsed.

Ever since 2020, Puerto Rico has been experiencing violent earthquakes which, although mostly confined to the south, did occur on multiple occasions in our north coast precisely above the municipality of Arecibo. I remember when the news came out that day, there were reports of an earthquake in the north. I just hope that for the sake of my island we can rebuild/reuse that space for scientific research.

Very interesting, and very sad. I'd love to see a follow-up some time detailing what has been learned, and for me to explain why this seemingly entirely inexplicable and unnecessary series of events happened: To me this aging telescope had a cable failure, but the response wasn't "replace the cable if we can afford to do so". Instead a series of seemingly entirely superfluous committees and studies were instituted to discuss the how, why, and when, of the situation. Ok, but why delay repairs?  In the meantime there was a cascade of increasingly serious failures that only resulted in yet more discussion rather than action, to the point that the observatory has likely been lost for good. Why? I realise the issue is likely to be a lack of understanding on my part rather than mismanagement, even if it looks like that to me. But to my 'uneducated' eye this was a very straightforward problem that got out of control as a result of insisting on answering questions for which there were either existing answers or asking questions that didn't need answering at the time, instead of getting on with fixing the problem. eg: -Oh, dear. A support cable has broken. Do we replace it? Of course, there is no debate here!!! Cost could be the only reason not to do so. (closure) -How do we replace it? Really!?! Not only was the structure built 60 years ago, extra cables we added fairly recently. The answer is already known. -Is it safe? It will certainly get more unsafe the longer repairs are delayed, so let's get on with it! Safety assessments can be made during the time taken to arrange men and materials to do the initial repair. -Now we once again have a stable and hopefully safe structure we have time to discuss 'what next?' to our heart's content. See my point? So what am I missing? Why was the response seeming 'unnecessary talk and delay' instead of action?  If I hear one of the legs of a chair I'm sitting on 'crack', I get off the chair and see to making a repair. Assuming I think it's worth my while. I don't continue to sit on the chair while debating with my friends why it happened or how and when I should get around to fixing it, all the while observing ominous cracking noises coming from the other legs.

I thinking the addition of the Gregorian Dome is what played the most Factor in the failing of it.  Added all that extra weight even though they did add some extra support cabling to it. I dont think it was Enough. Yes it lasted a long time, but if the Dome wasnt added, I think it would have still be standing today.  Perhaps if 2 or 3 more Towers were Added and Cabling done. It might be completely fine with the added weight of the Gregorian Dome.    But with the Dome added with not much added as support, It was only a Matter of Time. Which the existing cabling and the newer one added for the dome. I think the Cabling had to much weight and was causing stress to it, which Lessened the Time frame on the cables. (IE Say a cables lifespan is 100 years either at or below its recommended weight strength limit.  Now, slowly increase weight to the cable above the weight strength limit. While it might stay at say 100 years for a little bit of added weight. when you get heavier and heavier weight added. Lifespan starts to go down and down until breaking point.  Yes I know its called something differently and how its done. Just trying to make it Simple and for people who arent a Engineer and or know alot of the Technical words for it. )((FYI Im not a Engineer either, But do know some of the stuff))

Ignoring maintenance crews is a measure to not want to spend money, plain and simple, failure is inevitable. It should have been designed with a retractable stand that the suspended load could be lowered to so the load could be taken off the cables and they could be inspected for stress fractures and corrosion. I have been a maintenance mechanic/electrician for 20 years. All cables at some point need replacement. The dang thing is in a hard to reach spot, a poor country, and a high humidity environment, which isn't good for steel structures. In my opinion, painting cables isn't as good as putting thick exposed gear grease on top of the paint for extra protection.

Okay, so who pays to clean up this mess?
About 20 years ago on a job off the southern Texas coast we were deploying a 3" cable (technically called wire rope) with the towing end secured with a spelter socket which in turn was secured to an onboard winch.  The onshore end was pre-rigged to a pipeline "sled" that was to be used in the lead for towing a new pipeline off of the beach and then into deep water for installation there for the oil & gas industry.  As the cable was being fed off the vessel when the "end" was reached the cable pulled right out of the socket and fell to the seabed.  Just the deployed weight of the wire rope alone had caused the socket to fail.  There were some wide eyes when the cable simply kept going straight off the stern.  Faulty installation/fabrication of the (factory) spelter socket was of course the cause.  A replacement cable later performed as required.
You'd think they'd have a way to lower the thing and take the tension off the cables to change them..

20 year intevals at maximum for no replacement of strained portions of mechanical supports. Cooling towers are exposed to accelerated weathering and corrosion, and those take a maximum of five years before something begins to wear out and potentially collapse. A telescope that requires decent precision to look and project into space cannot go that long without replacement/maintenance.

About 65 years ago, a local ham radio operator explained that every year he had to loosen the steel guy wires to keep his antenna straight. Reason: Rusting of the steel INCREASED the diameter and DECREASED the length. Further, he told me that the cables would break and his 70 foot high antenna would come crashing down. Could this be a factor in this crash ??

With all the news about Tofu Dreg projects in China, one must ask, was that first cable to pop out of it's socket made in China?  Also, it's somewhat amusing to see how any "expert" who is ever going to be brought in to create a guarantee of safety balk and say, "Sorry, we can't guarantee it will be safe."  The reality is that there is never a 100% guarantee.  Workers get distracted.  Somebody eating a banana tosses a peel on the ground.  Real keystone cops stuff, but it's responsible for far more injuries on job sites than inadequate design.  The reality is that no expert, who doesn't share in the upside or downside of the project, is going to guarantee safety.  They will not bear the cost of loss if the facility / project is abandoned.  Nor do they get any upside if the project goes forward successfully.  But they have tremendous downside if the project goes forward and is unsuccessful, so they will always have a distorted incentive to sandbag.  FYI, that's why we didn't get Woodstock 50 either. China has a quarter mile telescope and has joined as a founding member of SKA. America has no membership of SKA and now has no large radio telescope on that scale.  Assuming SKA goes online, America will be in danger of losing world status in radio astronomy, which will lead to money and talent going elsewhere.   Given the threat of irrelevance, an array of telescopes with a greater collecting area than Arecibo and superior resolution is essential. And this needs to be done before SKA is fully online.
Why the Arecibi Telescope structure failed was because they didn't use 4 points of connection instead they used only 3 basically putting support cable structures at the 12 3 6 9 positions of the structure to better distribute the load across 4 points instead of only 3 points.

This was not such a great loss for science as most people think. The fixed structure of the radio telescope, the age of the instruments, and the development of much superior radio telescopes elsewhere made the Arecibo radiotelescope not worth the money it took to run it, let alone to refurb and upgrade the site. Arecibo's scientific use had pretty much stopped decades before the collapse. It was scheduled to be demolished all the way back in 2006 but just enough funding to keep it running (but not upgrade or maintain it) was brought in from other sources besides the US government. Still, its usefulness was still low and would have probably been removed after the hurricanes. The collapse just speeded up the process.
It was originally a cold war military installation, what did you expect? It wasn't meant to serve as a radiotelescope for multiple decades- it wasn't meant to last, yet it did. Demolishing the thing was probably the best possible option, since it greatly exceeded its service life. Nature was simply quicker.
What really happened? Lack of maintenance. Not difficult.

Several factors are involved including the age of the structure but the most significant was the additional load of the new Gregorian dome and its associated instrumentation. The tie down cables to stabilize the dome added more load, none of this was part of the original engineering design. In all likelihood the original configuration would still be standing but the structure would probably be closing in on its useful service life a few years or perhaps a decade down the road. The engineering team's recommendation to tear down the structure and cordon off a safety zone until that task was completed was spot on and they should be commended for a brilliant evaluation. Time to build a replacement system that's bigger and better.

A cable supported structure usually has some rigorous maintenance, and not just slapping on another coat of paint.  Antenna towers have guy tensions measured periodically, and tower alignment checked to confirm integrity.  Even the Gold Gate Bridge has routine cable inspections, and while visual inspections are not like Superman looking thought the cable with X-Ray vision,  there are known things to look for which show that there is a problem beginning.  Apparently, nobody was paying attention in PR.

As I was watching this, one thing came to mind about the tension of the cables. It was the soda can effect. The cables had been bearing the original weight if the satellite reciever for the longest time and when the dome was added, it added more stress to the cables, weakening them even further so the tension in the cables was not recalibrated to additional weight nor were they replaced to accommodate it.  So if you step on an the empty can, it won't crush but as soon as you put a straw in your hand, step on, it gets crushed.  This is what I believe happened. In the end, it took longer for failure to occur after the extra weight was added.

When I was a teen in the mid-late 2960s, my family lived in Puerto Rico. One Sunday after church, my father had the great idea of driving to Arecibo to see the telescope. It was a blistering hot tropical day and we were in a car with no air conditioning that felt like an oven. Marques says the drive should take about an hour but, whether from suffering or not, my memory was that it took many hours. When we got there, nothing was open because it was Sunday, and there was nothing to do but stair down at this valley covered with metallic netting.  As a teen who had no interest in space exploration at the time, it was one of my least enjoyable experiences of the time. It's just a very bad memory for me. Of course, that was over 50 years ago and I would love to visit today, albeit in an air conditioned car. But just seeing or hearing the word "Arecibo" can make me shudder sometimes.  It was

In my head i see it as a problem of too many parallel cables. The real strength of an ordinary rope, organic, synthetic or metal, is the twisted (or braided) nature of its individual strands and fibers. Although technically this reduces their max strength a little, it makes them able to share the load much more evenly as a bigger structure. If you have a rope consisting of 20 separate fibers it will be strong, but if they were taken apart and placed in a parallel line the rope will fail extremely fast, because of the small differences in length and strength, and also minor damage. It would start a chain event similar to the footage of the cables snapping until every fiber is torn. Of course this was probably not the real reason for the failure as the engineers knew what they were building, and adjusted everything carefully to be balanced.  But maybe after 60 years of use that balance was skewed, and cables at only 60 percent (ideally) loading were over as much as 200 percent?

This is a fantastic video. As someone who works in the research community that used Arecibo, I can say that you have accurately and fairly assessed this event.  Some additional considerations is that there was an uptick in earthquake activity in the region after the hurricanes, which likely added additional dynamic forces that stressed the cables. In fact, a cable had to be repaired after an earthquake a decade ago. Interestingly, there was an earthquake nearby on the date of and at the time of the collapse. This might be what finally pushed it over the inevitable edge.
Another failure mode nobody wants to talk about is the age, I'm pretty sure it wasn't designed with a 57 year lifespan in mind. That is a lot of heating/cooling cycles and the constantly shifting stresses from wind.

The dynamic loading of each suspended would or should have been calculated as a compound live load rather than a static load which is typically 50 times the expected capacity if people are within the sphere of the project. Looking at the design and your own comments suggest that the entire structure was substantial under-designed to handle the original structure let alone the massive upgrades that followed. A curious question: How often were the cables replaced? Even the expansion bridges require constant maintenance and even replacement of those cables after a certain period of time. My guess is that the structure was built outside of the United States because it did not meet the stringent safety standards demanding such high safety protocols. Even basic safety harness equipment requires a minimum of 50,000 pounds per square inch attachment points per person on construction sites where an individual is more than 5 feet off the ground, with the exception of ladders up to 10 feet of the ground or inside a contained area such as walls. I have seen the Arecibo project a number of times over the years and wondered exactly how they could actually detect the level of corrosion in those fastening anchors  because once they are sealed and installed there is no practical way to physically inspect the individual strands of cable inside the wedge anchors. Electrolysis would be a naturally occurring event with or without the influence of sea salt corrosion and the constant affects of weather. I am inclined to believe that the entire project team was fooled by their achievements much like everyone was convinced that the Titanic couldn't be sunk. Throughout history we have seen the same scenario unfold time and again because a project is so spectacular or has been working flawless for so long that short-sightedness creeps in, much like the Florida condominium disaster recently that exposed several similar era buildings showing the same types of issues, causing a critical review of many buildings across the country. It is very sad to see the loss of this amazing feat of engineering, but the very mention of financial issues indicates that proper maintenance and management were also key factors that most definitely played a part. üò¢ While no-one ever wants to admit such truths, science is always underfunded and most projects struggle to show their viability over time, sad, but true.

A promoted user comment at Ars Technica is beyond superb. See _NSF releases footage from the moment Arecibo‚Äôs cables failed_  by John Timmer from December 2020. Here's the gist:   "When Arecibo was built, the receiver that detects the signal and the digitizers were incredibly expensive. So to get a lot of sensitivity, you built one big dish ... and focused it on one expensive receiver + digitizer, which were housed in the white ball ... These have been upgraded over the years (liquid helium cooled amplifiers) and the fantastic digital spectrometers ... But they are much less expensive now, so you can build arrays of dishes with many receivers and digitizers. ... On a science/$ basis a rebuilt Arecibo is not very competitive. This is really down to the cost of receivers and digitizers falling so much that arrays of equivalent cost have greater science range."  So yes, you can future-proof a facility with a 3√ó or 11√ó safety margin, but you _can't_ future proof a facility over a fundamental change in the nature of the most cost-effective design mode.   When it's big and cool we demand an engineering margin that will last 100 years out of doors in the blustery tropics. But if it's small and slim (e.g. your iPhone) most people are perfectly happy to landfill the gadget every 24 months in pursuit of the latest and greatest nano-feature. My old phone that lasted 10 years had a perfectly good calculator app from the stone age. You have the power. But as Smokey Bear used to say on behalf of the Forest Service, only you can do the math.

CORROSION KILLS - On salt water laden radio installations at various points - especially where there is a possibility of dissimilar metals in mutual contact being used (electrolytic corrosion) - I drown the parts in Copper Colloidal grease called Copperease (commercial name) this rejects salt laden moisture, and does not wash off or emollify, it also lasts for donkey's years. It‚Äôs a high temperature grease as well used in the automotive trade. I was introduced to using it on ship work as opposed to using sheep fat grease by an engineer who designed seaborne pirate radio station medium wave antennas in the 60s. Impractical probably for any work here and far too late. Paint did not prevent water ingress. That's a good point... shock loading a cable significantly reduces it's static capacity. Any climber knows this. So I think that's a good line of investigation. The first cable probably had some unseen defect in it or had been (perhaps with all the cables) over stressed by one or more hurricanes, and when it gave way, it overloaded the rest of the cables but not beyond the breaking point. Enough, though, to reduce the remaining safety margin to critical levels, not to mention that the fact of its being missing trimmed the safety margin just by itself. When the second cable broke, it probably did the same thing but in that case, it dropped the holding strength of the remaining cables below the required minimum. A few good gusts of wind and a little time was all that was needed to exceed the breaking strength of the remaining cables.

The fastenings on elevator hoist ropes in the US and Canada now typically use "wedge rope sockets" which have largely replaced babbitted sockets which used molten metal or thermosetting resin as the embedment medium. In babbitted sockets used on elevators, the ends of the ropes were first seized with a circumferential wire wrapping where they entered the socket. The individual wraps of the rope were separated and the fiber core was removed within the socket. Then the ends were bent over and this "rose" was pulled into the shackle. This would make it impossible for the rope with a now greatly increased diameter, to pull through the end of the shackle. The babbitt held everything together. Elevator hoist ropes are also of a different construction than those at Arecibo in that they are designed to constantly flex. Additionally, and perhaps more critically they are indoors and normally protected from the environment.

In my opionion, the videos misses one possible cause for the rupture of the second (one of the original) cables. The cable was installed in the 60ies, so about 60 years of service life, give or take. Now we know that cable structures are susceptible to vibration, swinging etc., hence load cycles. And even if the stress ranges are small, we're talking about 60 years of life, so many cycles. Depending on the type of cable, other strands may bridge this gap, but if more and more strands brake, there's a natural limit. And don't let codes and standards for fatigue design fool you: there is no natural limit where stresses below that limit do not cause damage. That's a technical decision and usually it's OK, as fatigue loaded structures are regularly checked. BTW, I don't assume that fatigue was even considered in the original design. Even today if would be hard. Wind turbines designed for 20 years of service life run into the giga cycle fatigue, so billions of load cycles. And there is no real code to actually make a good design, as most fatigue testing is done in the range of up to 5-10 million cycles, as everything above takes time and is too costly. And for most of the structures that range is quite OK.

What really happened was the structure was not designed to be maintained. The cables were designed in such a way that they were permanently anchored and the tension could not be released in a controlled manner to allow their periodic replacement. It was a bad design and that is ultimately what doomed the telescope to a finite lifespan. The cables should have been on winding drums to allow the tension to be relieved in a controlled fashion and individual cables replaced.

It lasted 60 years in a highly corrosive area.  It did pretty well for a guyed structure in that environment with Hurricane force winds imo.  A safety factor of 2 is acceptable for standard structural design.  At that time I'm sure they used ASD but with cables you don't really have a design corrosion loss of steel like standard steel members.  Due too it being stranded there is much more area for the corrosion to happen.  The 60% failure is likely directly related to steel loss due to corrosion.  Today we would coat the cables or shield them (as you alluded to).  The paint is only mildly useful for corrosion in that environment.

Great information!  I'm sure that fatigue has some part to play in it. When you design with a factor of safety of 2 you are already going to be approaching it's long term fatigue strength, and when you add up the number of cycles of stress due to wind over 50 years, and then you add the load of the vertical stabilizer lines, it becomes a recipe for disaster. The sad part is that many bridges around this country are going to have the same problem, because they are allowing them to rust heavily and they are being weakened until they will fall soon. Thank you for this very interesting and even MORE informative presentation. The structure, itself, was an amazing bit of engineering and derring-do construction from a time in our history when that was not all that unusual. After all, we were taking WWII technology and building new machines derived therefrom to use to send men to the moon. What is surprising is that all of that added structure and weight were added to the original structure with a minimalist approach to re-enforcing the original structure to support it. That is continued to stand for as long as it did thereafter is a testament to the strength built into the original design. It is noteworthy that the  Chinese decided to build one that is 60% larger in diameter. Is there a need for this kind of technology today or are there better, more efficient and sensitive technologies that could do as well or even better at a lower cost of construction and operation?

Looks like the countersink flange went out on the CD-7 unit multiplier, knocking out hydraulic torque to the electric heat riser. Most countersink flanges will experience hydraulic torque leak throughout their less than 7,000 RQMs. Especially when coupled with the double-flux lumen switcher. But with Wilson Countersink Flanges and Dorry Flanges, hydraulic torque is allowed to bypass the settling clutch, providing steady wall pressure to the lug manifold and all the seismic rotors. And that goes for 7,000 RQMs, 8,000 RQMs, even up to 10,000 RQMS. The question was never really could it have been done safely. You would never have been able to repair it safely. That was always going to be a high risk job even if they had the safety margin the engineers wanted. Some jobs just aren't safe and this would have been one of them. The question was always going to be the cost to repair it. They didn't want to spend the money and spent too much time analyzing it.   1) It doesn't look like there was an easy way to replace the cables until they broke. Why did no one think of a way to replace the cables while in place when the telescope was designed? 2) It wasn't hard to see that after that first cable broke that the possibiity of one of the other cables could very well fail. The forces on the other cables when the platform dropped would have overloaded their max weight. If they were going to fix it they should've installed a new cable immediatly 3) Were there no emergency cables stored onsite so one could be replaced immediatly if one broke? If not then why not? It seems like common sense to have at least one or two replacement cables onsite just in case one broke.   These are just a couple of things engineers should consider if they plan on replacing this telescope with a new one. Hopefully they do. There are still a lot more discoveries to be made.

Would also like to point out that the Arecibo observatory remained the largest RADAR telescope in the world, as the chinese FAST dome is only a radio telescope that passively collects radio waves. Arecibo was capable of sending radio waves out and observing them. This why we were able to determine the composition of Jupiter's atmosphere, by bouncing radio waves off of it. Here's hoping they can put a modern structure that replicates the function of the original, iconic observatory there one day.

Perhaps a bit of an oversight on the original design (probably due to cost and limited expectation of increasing the load) was to not have the ability to lower the heavier suspended elements from the supporting cable structure.  So the cable sets would be affixed to a sub or hoisting frame, and then the rotational unit and instrument packages could be lowered to some fixed struts projecting up through the dish for the service of all the components.  Assuming the towers themselves weren't being overloaded, individual cables could replace with heavier ones, or additional cables could be added more safely.  Perhaps a thought for any future dishes.  To design such a large and potentially problematic structure with some significant degree of serviceability built in is pretty short sighted.  (Though as an engineer, I am constantly pressed to spend less money on a project; I have never had one that would have had such a catastrophic, and therefore expensive, failure mode.)

High-cycle fatigue ? The wire rope would have been subjected to variations in tension around the static tension, so a +/- fatigue cycle on top of the big + static tension. Furthermore, there could have been internal abrasions in the individual strands against each other causing surface roughening of each strand and reducing its cross-sectional area.

I think it's fair to say the hypothetical cable flaws are one way to see this loss but the other way is to see the design was flawed from the outset.  Better cables won't compensate for weak anchors and stronger anchors won't compensate for improperly supported mass.  There was virtually no reserve strength or redundancy in the cable architecture.  The oldest bridges in the world are precisely those which were overbuilt.  This was built on a scarecrow diet.

Another consideration of the strength of the original Aricebo design is the circumstances of it's creation. In the late 59s there were no reliable systems for detecting missle launches in a timely manner. The Department of Defense was funding many possible scientific proposals and evaluating them for deployment. Aricebo was built quickly to address a present threat. A maintenance program was only created after the fact.
Aliens‚Ä¶‚Ä¶‚Ä¶they took out our radar in preparation of the invasion.
"Cables were regularly painted" Cables are not supposed to be painted, they should be left bare but impregnated with grease. This keeps out water, inhibits corrosion and lubricates between stands as they shift under varying load. The Aricebo cables were originally bare and greased, but then when a James Bond scene was to be filmed there on the suspended antenna, the directors insisted that the cables had to be painted white for appearance in the movie. The site management allowed that. And so the telescope's fate was sealed - by a James Bond film shoot. The paint prevented keeping the cables properly grease-filled. They began corroding. Expansion due to corrosion places uneven loads on different strands...

If it's possible, I would wanna see a new telescope built.  One with aluminum panels that aren't rigidly placed (maybe the whole surface could be folded up like an origami flower and stored for when storms are on the way or the telescope isn't in use), and a receiver which can be flown like a drone rather than suspended from cables (or somehow raised and lowered rather than staying rigidly in one place all the time).  The panels could even be set up as a series of hexagons rather than a solid piece.  This would mean setup and removal takes longer, but it's safer overall for everyone and everything involved because there's not so much weight to deal with, and any damage that *is* sustained is localized.  The main issue I see here is that the cables were installed in such a way that replacement was already difficult at best, impossible at worst.  Being able to raise and lower the main load (the platform) would likely allow the telescope to keep going for many years to come, and with the delicate panels underneath able to be folded up and removed, this could reasonably be achieved in any of a variety of ways, even if the platform is too heavy to fly.  But hey, it's possible that I don't know enough about the subject to know what I'm talking about.  It's just an idea.  :D

The Arecibo Telescope lasted for a long time, only because it was built in a different era. In those days maintenance was still a thing, because people valued what Arecibo meant and could do. But as technology advanced and at some point surpassed what Arecibo was capable of, interest waned and funding and therefore maintenance declined, until finally the breaking point was reached. Very nice video. And I was surprised by a couple of things: spelter sockets normally are filled with resin, rather than with molten zinc, but that was not available back in the days yet. Furthermore, components like sockets, shackles, etc typically have a factor of safety of 4 or 5 these days, much higher than during construction of the facility. Given the conditions and modifications, I am actually suprised that it was able to stay in tact for so long.
True story of my experience of engineers - We built an octagonal retaining wall for an engineer once. The engineer saw us doing this, was a bit amused, and told us he would figure it the correct way for us. 1:40 this is incorrect. angular resolution is determined by area, as per the Rayleigh criterion. Exposure time affects sensitivity and signal to noise ratio, not resolution. you could focus a radio telescope on a spot in the sky for 100 years and still not be able to resolve whether an object is two closely-spaced stars or if one large star, whereas a telescope with a larger dish (or two telescopes spaced apart from each other) could do so almost immediately.  I know this is practical engineering and not practical astronomy or practical optical physics, and that this video is simplified for the layman, but treating exposure time and aperture size as two interchangeable qualities that can be swapped around to achieve the same thing is more than a simplification, its false.

Two highly relevant words that are missing from presentation, Metal Fatigue. The constant pulling and release of tension, over the years, played a part in this collapse. Like a spring in a magazine, over time, being compressed, and decompressed, eventually leads to failures to load the cartridge properly, known as Fail To Feed in the shooting sports. The constant shifting of weights and tension that occurs when changing location and focusing distance, over time, had a significant role I would imagine. Sad loss regardless, Perhaps a carbon fiber cable and/or structural skeleton might help alleviate the load weight in a rebuild?

Small correction: Measuring for longer doesn't improve the resolution, it improves the "light" yield (and integrated signal amplitude), but the resolution is defined by the wave length and aperture according to the Rayleigh criterion. You can't get above that no matter how long you measure. However, this is why telescope arrays are useful. The effective area isn't necessarily larger than a single dish, but their aperture is defined by the distance between telescopes (which for example in the extreme case of the EHT that took the black hole image was the diameter of the earth).

A little something about FAST to add to the immense importance of Arecibo. FAST one and only claim to fame is that it is bigger. Arecibo's uniquely powerful RADAR capability allowed us to range space bodies like asteroids and planets with unmatched accuracy. It's lost means looking for asteroid based threats has become a lot harder.  A big dish now days isn't the singular defining advantage. It's now all about Very Long baseline interferometry. VLBI allows you to have a "Dish" as large as the most distant receivers. This is how the event Horizon telescope works, resulting in a "Dish" as large as the Earth. This requires extremely accurate clocks, massive computational power for data sets so massive they have to ship it. It is also why when you see other radio telescopes(Goldstone) it's not one dish, it is an array of dishes.  Another titbit about FAST is that it hasn't produced anything notable, not because others have already done it, it lacks the expert staff to run it. The number of people who can run such an observatory is extremely limited. Well before the current tensions, other issues meant doing science there was untenable, like not being able to share data is an immediate dealbreaker. FAST wasn't built for science, it's for propaganda. Arecibo Cold War aside was built for a functional purpose.

FYI, a NASA report on the initial socket failure is available at https://ntrs.nasa.gov/citations/20210017934 . The executive summary seems to basically state that the socket was insufficiently dimensioned, and that in hindsight there had been visible signs of cable pull-out from the socket. It also states that the likely reason for the subsequent cable failure was, indeed, that the factor of safety was much too low to remain standing (or hanging, as the case may be) on the remaining cables, so the conclusion of the video seems very correct!

As a metallurgist, my first guess was stress corrosion cracking. In the production of steel itself, way back when original cables were installed, the casting techniques may not be as sophisticated to avoid bifilms (mainly folding of the oxidized surface layers onto themselves while pouring the liquid melt) within the steel. I say this because even the term bifilms didn't exist back then. Anyway. And at the time of installment, the test results were correct because the corrosive media had not penetrated the bifilms yet. Also pouring hot zinc onto steel wires would surely make a good bond and you acheive the  required strength in the joint. But the interface between the wire and the zinc may not be impervious to corrosive media and here since the metals are different, there also exist a possibility of galvanic corrosion if you provide an electrolyte at the interface. The inteface may also be compromised because the coefficient of thermal expansion for both metals are different. Usually this may not be a problem but if you hold things in tension for about 50 years in a costal area where corrosion is a big factor, you should be careful. Anyway this is almost anyone's guess. Let's see the final report whenever it comes.

The NSF has a lot to consider, but my vote would to be to abandon any consideration of rebuilding a fixed dish at Arecibo.  Hurricanes will forever plague the site, and given the UHF through HF bandwidth of the large aperture, local RF noise from cell phones to LED lights will be difficult to control and nearly impossible to shield against.  As much as I'd love to see Puerto Rico get a new telescope, I'd be the first to say that it would be a very poor use of funds for the high maintenance and science return on investment.  Take a look at China's FAST telescope, and look at the multiple difficulties that RF noise is creating for the facility.  They are actually considering sacrificing science for tourism dollars.
Nothing is for eternity. A sad truth. Let's hope the successor will be just as good or better.

Well-considered analysis.  My hope is that the lessons learned can be applied to similar cable-supported structures world-wide.  For instance, if the first failure was in the socket, what were the causal factors and how can we avoid them or inspect for them in the future?  It's interesting that the dehumidification system lacked the essential exterior cable jacket...my assumption is that detail greatly reduced the expected moisture-removal effectiveness.  Finally, regarding factors of safety, I agree that such a unique and critically difficult structure to inspect and repair should have had a much higher cable and socket factor of safety, perhaps as high as 5.  That said, the DOD may not have designed the facility for a 60 year lifespan...all structures have a design life and I would be interested to know what the original design life was.

This pickiness is just because you're an engineer, understand:    - 1:06 The graphic implies a change in amplitude rather than a change in frequency, as would be proper.    - 1:36 Not sure extended signal input with time equates to a higher *resolution* .  Even if accumulation over time consequently improves the ability to pick out weak signals, that doesn't seem to be within the technical definition of that word.    - 2:14 Detection of nuclear weapons tests to monitor proliferation is a peaceful pursuit.  Detonation of such weapons is not.    - 2:37 "Most iconic".  What are the units of measurement for that?  This is an essentially meaningless phrase that has infested writing in the last few years.    - 2:49 RADAR is an acronym for "radio detection and ranging" but the example given is of radio-frequency imaging, instead.  Extra point for correct pronunciation of Spanish "a" sound.  Much appreciate your videos.  Keep 'em coming.  Cheers.

The structure outlived its design goal 6x, so if there is anything to blame, it is the fact it has been left serving nearly to these days and not been dismantled and completely rebuild decades ago. Don't forget it was designed as a military observing post, so things designed for about 10 years of lifetime. So the factor of 2 safety was good enough - with such expected lifetime they could completely disregard things like the corrosion or so. Yes, 10 years after build, the military just wrote it off and handed it to the civilian research, to serve there few more years. It is at that time, when the device had proven its scientific value, when it should had been dismantled and the main structure redesigned for way longer service life (so take e.g. the corrosion effects, or the ability to replace the structural components into account). But that was deemed too expensive for something the scientific community got for free to start with.  But hope this event will lead to the complete new, better mainainable structure for the future being build. The site is in high value for this, as mainly the population living around is already used to and adopted to the limitations such device imposes on their life (restriction on activities generating disturbances in the radio spectrum), this day this would be very problematic to achieve that anywhere else, so it is asset which should not be lost there...

My simple observation is that every steal string snaps close to the point it is welded to the spelter socket. What is clearly visible with that is that the socket, with a half meter of cable, is poorly  available for maintenance, and that while it is the most important part of the cable. You can see that the already broken cable leaves a unpainted surrounding in the sleeve in the metal block, and that while this part IS accessible for painting. Further inside the sleeve, paint can't come, but water will. Such a construction should be fully open for maintenance, or regularly injected with or submerged in something like silicon grease.

Without millimetre by millimetre inspection of the cables for micro-fractures, sectional testing of the metal from the cables no one can say with absolute certainty the cables met the designed strength and quality. No doubt both are part of the ongoing investigations, the blame game to be able to say it was this that caused it not our use being a driving factor.  There is a cable suspension bridge locally that is far older than Arecibo, actually crosses salt water so even more heavily exposed to the salt air. The first visual inspection of the cables was in the early 2000s, they were rust free. The engineer actually set up a perfect system to protect the steel from the sea air, wrapping them in waxed paper, then cedar, then that sealed inside more steel. The load bearing material completely isolated from the corrosive salty air. It was built shortly after the Golden Gate was, with no loss of life.

I think  that the basic cause is the increment of weight slowly brought. "Can we add this equipment ? It would be a great improvement of the capacities, and it only weighs 1 % of the mass ?"  Then, pressure of several influent organisations so : "EUH, we are not fans, but OK !" In France, the Tour Eifel had many "improvements" of that sort, some for useful technical aspects, some for commercial reasons like toilets and restaurants for visitors. But at the end, some intelligent guy said "Enough of all that, remove everything added". The argument was powerful because Tour is in the middle of Paris and had many visitors and attached business. So it was finally accepted that it was "too much" and modifications and removings done while complete overhaul executed. Perhaps such a hard decision would have rescued ARECIBO. But it seemed evident that this enormous station and radome wind sensitiveadded was an incredible and enormous risk. Consequences were deadly, not for any man, fortunately.

I lived on the site for 6 months as a budding astrophysicist back in the late 1980s. It was a glorious place, and oh, that thing was so sensitive! And it just got better in the 1997 upgrade.¬†  But alas, it was approaching the end of its scientific life span anyways. It made major contributions, but the science has moved on and now requires different telescopes for different questions. It still had some life left in it as the most sensitive planetary radar for NASA's Sentry near-Earth asteroid detection program, but its scientific heyday was in the past.  I will miss it dearly.

I'm not a structural engineer, but from every video I've watched on structural collapses I'd be prepared to bet that the increased dynamic loads caused by wind/weather were either underestimated or not accounted for at all when the Gregorian dome was added. The auxiliary cables bore the increased static load within the safety margin, but the increased vibrations and stresses caused by strong winds acting on the dome accelerated the main cables to early failure. The loss of the tie-down cable is a possible clue, although it sounds more likely that this was due to a manufacturing or installation fault re. the spelter socket. Nonetheless, I'm pretty positive that the collapse was due to a design oversight that took many decades to reveal itself. Thanks for the excellent video Grady!

Its designed factor of safety was only 2?  Damn!  I studied Mining Engineering and worked at Creighton Mine (which is also home to a neutrino observatory called SNOLAB), that gave me a pretty solid understanding of when it's acceptable to use low factors of safety.  In mines, we get as low as a FS of 1.2 for autonomous parts of the mine - that is: no human will ever enter there, only remote controlled vehicles.  1.5 is considered acceptable for human access to temporary workings - the individual work tunnels and such that live out their usefulness within a few years.   More permanent structures, like the mineshaft that provides access to the entire mine, are generally given an FS around 2-3.  (SNOLAB's FS is much higher)    And this works because mines are intended to be temporary structures, accessed only by trained personnel who can report problems as soon as they occur, and subject to frequent maintenance inspections.  And all of their equipment is just off the shelf stuff - if there is an emergency the only thing in the mine that's not replaceable is the people working down there  (Except SNOLAB, which again, was in a much more carefully designed area).    Arecibo was a unique scientific instrument.  Its radar capabilities were unique and may well have proven critical to humanity's survival had a substantial asteroid impact been coming during the period it was operational (the data from such images would be very useful in determining a method of destroying or redirecting the asteroid in question such that it would miss Earth). Most of it was suspended high in the air, and difficult for maintenance crews to reach.  Nobody would be able to see minor problems coming except during maintenance because the towers were not areas traversed by the people working there on a daily basis.  And building a new one is going to be a redesign from the ground up.

Cables or wire ropes, as we call them in my profession, are a type of machine. And like any other machine, they can fail. When working with draglines, shovels or cranes the wire ropes are frequently inspected and often require replacing.  I'm certain they require a lot of maintenance but it is amazing to me that the cables employed to support structures as the Arecibo Telescope, bridges, radio transmission towers, etc. are able to last decades after decades without having to be replaced. It is such a tragic loss and yet so incredibly ironic that such a complex instrument can be destroyed by the failure of such a simple machine. I hope resources become available to reconstruct the telescope and its amazing journey is continued.

I agree that it should have had a higher safety factor. I have some experience with large cable arrays (though not as a structural engineer); some of them were from structures of almost the same age as this telescope. The inherited wisdom is that lines _will_ fail, and it's recognized that you're unlikely to have funding to replace them before they do (or are noticeably close to it). The cyclic loading combined with the environment guaranteed that those lines would lose strength over time, and it's impressive they lasted as long as they did (a testament to wire rope). The best chance to save the telescope was when they added the additional cables to support the new sensor array, since they were already adding cables. Unfortunately, hindsight is 20:20, and at the time, they probably considered the original design and beefed it up by an amount that would support the new structure, not keeping in mind the low safety factor in the original design. Or maybe they considered it, but didn't have the funding available to add an additional two cables per tower.

I likewise hope that they're able to install another megastructural instrument there to replace it - it's a great site and I hope it continues doing research for many years to come (with or without a new dish).  Point of order, though: FAST is a bigger telescope, but lacks Arecibo's transmission capacity - it's designed to be receive-only and the retrofit would be complex, as I understand it.  Humanity has lost a powerful space-radar - we have exactly one other comparable instrument (the Goldstone Solar System Radar in California, which has a transmission capacity barely a fifth of Arecibo's weakest transmitter and is significantly less sensitive).  It's not hyperbole, I think, to say that the Arecibo Dish was a unique facility, and that even 58 years after its construction it had no equal while it stood.

It should be pointed out that the original engeering did not fail. It was the engineering to add extra weight for the Gregorian dome that failed. When the new engineering failed, the old engineeing was pushed right to it's safety limit. 15:42  A safety factor of two sounds almost negligent, if that's really the factor for "the expected _static_ loading from the platform"...and no one in their right mind should have assumed the structure would have no dynamic loads, considering it's all exposed to the weather, in an area that often gets tropical storms if not hurricanes.

One of those awesome collaborations between engineering and science.
I would hope the cables weren't made as shown at 14:37.  Steel cables with a fiber core have a habit of corroding from the inside, with the fiber wicking moisture into the centre of the cable.  This would make them very difficult to inspect, as the strength will be seriously reduced long before there's any corrosion visible from the outside.  (Obvs, this can still be a problem with steel-core cables, but it's generally less common without having the fiber to act as a wick.)
Another pertinent observation here: John Agustus Roebling apparently wintered near me in Clearwater, Florida; there are a couple streets named for him.  He was the engineer who designed the Brooklyn Bridge, still open to traffic 138 years after it was first opened in the late 1800s.  I read somewhere that he was asked why that bridge was still standing, when so many from that era had collapsed.  His reply was that, not knowing what he didn't know, he calculated the maximum combined static and dynamic loads he could envision the bridge having to withstand...  And then multiplied by *six*.  That's engineering, folks; not seeing how far you can cut the design back.

Excellent as always, Grady. Impeccably researched, approachably explained, well edited... one of the few channels where I can let my scientific skepticism relax.  I've always thought, as both a scientist and engineer myself, that while scientists may be the ones pushing the forefront of human knowledge, and thus require the _deepest_ understanding of their fields, they're subject to making certain simplifying assumptions so regularly and for so much of their lives, that they often start to confuse those assumptions with reality. Some assumptions are especially insidious in that they're implicit, and not consciously accounted for. Thus, over the decades of a career, especially in theoretical science, those implicit assumptions quietly weave themselves into the tapestry of "what reality is" for each scientist.  Engineers, on the other hand, are mostly confined to work well within the bounds of well-understood science, and often have the _broadest_ understanding of their field. An engineer simply _cannot_ get lost in a world of simplifying assumptions. Every assumption has to come with some extra margin of design or safety to compensate in case it's wrong. In the most demanding cases, this can be extremely difficult, as the scope of such assumptions becomes very tiny and nuanced. If an engineer gains a bad habit of conflating any assumptions with reality, reality will give them a swift and unforgiving kick to the behind... often by dropping a bridge, a building, or a priceless instrument and artifact of the hope and curiosity of our species, at the cost of lives and, too often, a portion of our spirit.

Repetitive stress fatigue. As a motorcycle mechanic I know that control cables like a clutch cable are remarkably similar. And when they fail it looks very similar the failure in the video. They always break right at the joint. By the way an even better way to form a spelter socket is to heat the steel strands right at the end of the cable enough to melt the steel forming little enlarged balls on the ends of the strands. You just have to be careful to keep the heat as close to the end of the cable as possible as heating the cable steel above red hot embrittles it.

You should also never use a rotating rope or cable in a socket, only a non-rotating rope. Otherwise they can unwind out of the socket over time. We discontinued using sockets on rotating ropes in mining about 20 years ago. For hoisting in Shaft, single ropes use a FS of 5 at the sheave and 8 at the conveyance.  For multi rope hoists, a FS of 4 is used.  2 is pretty low FS for cables, even static.

Even if S was 1, if it failed at 0.62, there has to have been an introduced defect, either at fabrication or during use (e.g. fatigue traction/contraction due to the storms). Also, for those out there thinking S=1.5 is low for aviation, the space industry highest S is 1.2~1.25. Remember, in space, 1kg of extral material = thousands of extra $ in cost, and a .1 increase in S adds tons to the vehicle.

I think it is misguided to be critical of the initial design of the structure given its original purpose and the fact that it stood for 60 years. The DOD built it as an experiment and sold it once it was no longer useful to them. The fact that it lasted decades after its initial design purpose is a testament to the design.  There is a lot of debate about funding and how more money could have been thrown at upkeep of the structure. The sad fact is that there wasn't enough interest in the facility. Had there been a better PR campaign there could have been more government funding or even philanthropy funding (plenty of supposed philanthropic billionaires could have donated). Given this, failure was inevitable. Again, the facility stood decades after its original purpose, so the design wasn't as faulty as some people might believe.

Double margin was with new cable, likely with at least 30% extra from the cable manufacturer understating tensile strengths the cables were guaranteed to withstand.  However 60 years of spotty maintenance meant the paint was covering salt ingress, and thus making thousands of small electrochemical cells along the cable itself, leading to the gradual weakening of them. Eventually the strain was too great for the remaining cable, and you can see it was suffering from plastic deformation until it finally got to the yield point, and the rest went in a second or so as they were massively overloaded. Likely examination of the failed cable ends will show the plastic deformation and the corrosion cells where they failed in sequence, along with likely all of the cable length being corroded from the inside. The failed sockets likely a mix of the zinc being too cold when poured, the steel cable and socket was also not heated up enough, and 60 years of salt turning the zinc into zinc oxide and zinc chloride inside the joint.  And for elevators, the 11 times safety factor means a single cable is able to hold the car safely, even if all the others fail. Typically 4 cables minimum, though for long travel 6 is common, more to reduce wear on the sheaves than anything else, as this spreads the contact area out and thus the pressures are lower. Inspections not snapped strands, and replace them when there are too many snapped strands within a certain distance on the cable, or from an end. The quick way to look for snapped strands is to take a cloth, and wrap loosely around the cable, then run the car from top to bottom, and see if the cloth gets snatched. Almost no elevator accidents are due to cable failure, pretty much every other part fails first.

I'm sure each would provide only small lift, but maybe enough large ones could have compensated for the lost cables and allowed crews to safely attach emergency cables. Maybe even use drones to pull smaller cables over the existing supports and under the platform (allowing increasingly larger ones to be pulled over). Even if these were simply draped between existing cable mounts, it may have been enough for an emergency stopgap. Oh well.

14:34 Looks too familiar... The 3 brief years '79---'82 that I'd spent constructing passenger elevators (all but the music)... the hoisting cable's 6'' ends as shown; would be bent back toward the center of the cable like clutched fingers making a fist. The manila rope was then cut off @ the center. Then the running end of the cable was held to the floor by both feet & and with a very firm upward jerk on the 2' shackle; the fist was secured within each perspective shackle cup. From there it the cup was poured in place with hot melted babbitt. This guaranteed the bent cable strands would never escape the shackle cup while under tension. There would normally be 4 hoisting cables even though the rate of one single cable could cover the hoisting rated live load. But the single cable would be too springy & stretchy for a smooth ride. Therefore the other 3 cables turned the stretch into more of an single band We constructors were hated by all trades @ the beginning of the job, due to often taking up so much ground floor territory with our 16' long - 8# per foot hoist way rails (a real chore for a man on each end to navigate from the 18 wheeler to the hoist-way area opening) as well as their many wall mounting brackets, elevator car sling, cab crates, & temporary hoisting machines to suck those rails up the shaft to be stacked, one 128 lb.16 footer on top of another.  But once we had a cab-less running sling platform to all floors... why hell! Suddenly we were the most cherished of all trades for moving their sheet rock loads, water/sanitary sewer pipe & fittings, electrical conduit/fittings, wire rolls, but only on the week ends. Otherwise it was hit the stairs just like we did in order to get that running platform. Although it paid the average wage between the electricians, ironworkers, & plumbers (Hydraulic 2-7 floor machinery) it was dangerous work with a high chance of something being pulled off every floor into the hoist-way where we'd be setting doors/sills/conduit.

Your videos are amazing, and I know what I'm about to point out is nowhere near the point of this video, and that you're an engineer and not a physicist. But:  The EM spectrum at 1:14 is misleading. It appears to show changes in amplitude but not wavelength. Radio and IR are different because of wavelength (or frequency), not because of amplitude. The amplitude of light affects its intensity: if you increase the amplitude of visible light, you get brighter visible light -- not a new type of light.  A more accurate model would show all the waves with the same height (amplitude), but with the width (wavelength) starting out large on the left and getting tinier heading to the right.  Again, I know this is hardly relevant to the video's topic, but I teach middle school physical science, and hopefully someone will learn a lot from the video and benefit from this clarification. :)

I don't think you understand the function of the Tie Down cables. The receiver has to be at the correct distance from the dish to be in focus, but temperature changes during the day cause the primary support cables to sag, so the tie down cables would actively adjust and  reduce tension when things got hot to keep the receiver at the correct distance.

Arecibo, was one that got me into observatorys.
The success of the design principles mentioned here starting at 3:40 influenced the design of the telescope where I work. The HET at McDonald Observatory does with light and a spherical mirror what the Arecibo telescope did with radio waves and a spherical dish. The collecting apparatus at the top of our telescope (a set of correcting lenses in our case) also moves across our mirror, to provide the movement in elevation that the mirror doesn't have, and precise tracking in azimuth as well. The Arecibo telescope was clearly foundational for the development of our machine, and is mentioned in the papers that discuss the project as it was being originally conceived.

Great Video!  I think another aspect might be the general way buildings and structures were designed back in the day.   Whereas devices of that area typically were designed a lot more service friendly than devices today (which sometimes can't even be repaired by the manufacturer), the maintenance and accessibility of structures and buildings today is much improved and an integral part of the design process, whereas these aspects were more of an afterthought back in the day.   Getting that dish up there - mission accomplished. Contingency plans for maintenance, storms, getting that thing down again, safely replaceing those cables...not part of the spec book. "Well, we'll figure it out as we go" was more like it back in the day I guess, where everyone seemed a bit drunk by all the things they suddenly were able to achieve and people were feeling somewhat invincible with all that new tech. At least that's my impression when I enter older structures on jobs. Let's build a giant hall with a huge stage, yeah! Done! What? There is equipment that needs to get onto that stage? Whoopsiedoodles. Well....let's see, if we bastardize the orchestra pit and bang a hole into the wall down there, we can fabricobble some sort of load in access and drive that stuff to stage level via the hydraulic floor! Sure, the door is tiny because there wasn't planned anything but a manhole there and structural integrity is compromised by anthing wider than 90cm, and yes, the hydraulic floor will break two times a year, and yes, all this goes t*ts up if anything is set up in the pit, but there is always something and look at it, we made it happen wa?!? :D Unthinkable these days, but totally within the norm in concert halls of the 70s era.
